Paul Dirac Drive, Tallahassee, FL, 32310, United States ARTICLE INFO Keywords: Sodium-ion batteries Na 0.67 MnO 2 Liquid N 2 quenching Manganese vacancies Solid-state NMR spectroscopy ABSTRACT Mn-based layered oxides as one of the most promising and cost-effective cathode candidates for sodium-ion batteries still face great challenge to achieve high capacity with long cycle life under high-rate current simul taneously. In this work, we propose an effective strategy by a combination of liquid N 2 quenching and aliovalent doping to get new layered cathode materials. As evidenced by in-situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction, time-of-flight powder neutron diffraction and solid-state 23 Na nuclear magnetic resonance techniques, the proposed synthesis methods allow tuning the transition metal ions vacancies and enhance Mn 4 þ /Mn 3 þ redox center of P2-type Mnbased materials. Our results demonstrate that such an optimized structure significantly enhances the deliverable capacity, Na þ mobility and electronic conductivity of the materials. Furthermore, the effects of aliovalent doping elements and different cooling approaches on the long-range structure, local environment and electrochemical performance are comprehensively studied by comparing a wide range of doped Na 0.67 M x Mn 1-x O 2 (M 1⁄4 Li, Mg, Al, Fe) materials. The optimized Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.85 O 2 material exhibits a remarkably high initial capacity of 202 mAh g   1 among ever reported P2-type layered oxides within 2–4 V, a stable capacity retention of 81% after 600 cycles and outstanding rate capability of the specific capacity up to 122 mAh g   1 at 1200 mA g   1. 1. Introduction The rapid development of intelligent and environmental-friendly industrial technology, including smart mobile phone and green elec tric vehicles, has made rechargeable batteries an indispensable part of our modern society [ 1, 2 ]. Among various energy storage systems, lithium ion batteries (LIBs) with superior high energy and power den sities performance have accounted for the key position [ 3 ]. Neverthe less, the low reserve and uneven distribution of Li resources, and the use of high-cost and toxicity of Co and Ni as redox center for mostly LIBs cathode materials fail to fulfill demands of sustainable development [ 4 ]. Recently, sodium ion batteries (NIBs) have attracted many interests and are considered as the most promising candidate of LIBs, due to the fact that sodium possesses similar chemical properties and higher natural abundance than lithium [ 5, 6 ]. are considered as the most promising candidate of LIBs, due to the fact that sodium possesses similar chemical properties and higher natural abundance than lithium [ 5, 6 ]. In the development of NIBs, cathode is the most critical component that restricts cost and energy density in the full batteries [ 7, 8 ]. Comparing to polyanionic and tunnel-type cathodes materials for NIBs, layered transition metal oxides (Na x MO 2, M 1⁄4 transition metal) are extensively studied because of their high theoretical specific capacity * Corresponding author. State Key Laboratory for Physical Chemistry of Solid Surfaces, and Department of Chemistry, College of Chemistry and Chemical Engi neering, Xiamen University, Xiamen, 361005, People ’ s Republic of China. E-mail address: yyang@xmu.edu.cn (Y. Yang). Contents lists available at ScienceDirect Nano Energy journal homepage: http://www. elsevier.co m/locate/n anoen https://doi.org/10.1016/j.nanoen.2020.104997 Received 22 March 2020; Received in revised form 16 May 2020; Accepted 16 May 2020 Nano Energy 76 (2020) 104997 2 and fast Na þ mobility [ 9 ]. Among them, the low-cost Mn-based layered materials are deemed as ideal cathodes for large-scale energy storage. Two types of stacking structures, O3(ABCABC stacking) and P2(AABB stacking) are frequently observed in most of as-synthesized Na x MnO 2 materials within layered structure materials [ 10 ]. In comparison to the (de)sodiation of O3-type materials via the interstitial tetrahedral sites with relatively higher hopping energy from one octahedral site to another, the P2-type phase allows a direct migration between adjacent prismatic sites, thus enabling rapid Na þ transport and showing better applicability [ 11 ]. However, the available capacities of P2-type cathode materials are still insufficient yet [ 12, 13 ]. To address this issue, anionic redox reaction is introduced as its Li counterpart, which can be triggered to get extra capacity by charging the cells to a higher voltage range of 4.0–4.5 V vs. Na þ /Na (4.3–4.8 V vs. Li þ /Li). Unfortunately, the cathode materials inevitably suffer from electrolyte decomposition, multiple phase transition and even oxygen loss at such a high voltage, resulting in obvious voltage hysteresis and rapid capacity fade [ 14–17 ]. In contrast, controlling the cut-off voltage lower than 4 V for P2-type cathode can effectively eliminate aforementioned shortages and yield a much better cyclability, but the reversible capacity is hardly higher than 175 mAh g   1 [ 18–22 ]. Recent investigations have focused on the optimization of the crys talline/local structures and morphology engineering of Na x MnO 2 by adjusting the Na/Mn ratio, sintering temperature, cooling rate and aliovalent doping [ 20–25 ], which further determine the electrochemical performance. However, the overall performance of the materials is far below our expectation. The understanding of underlying structure-property relationship of the materials, especially the local environment including Mn vacancies, is insufficient for obtaining the material with high capacity and long cycle performance. The formation of Mn vacancies in the transition metal (TM) layers have been observed over 40 years [ 26–29 ]. Parant et al. proposed that Na 0.67 MnO 2 is able to uptake oxygen during the cooling process [ 27 ], resulting in the lift of Mn ions oxidation state and the formation of manganese deficient phases (denoted as □ ): Na 0.67 MnO 2 þ y/2 O 2 → Na 0.67 MnO 2 þ y (Na 0.67/(1 þ y/2) Mn 1/(1 þ y/2) □ (y/2)/(1 þ y/ 2) O 2 ) The thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) results of Dahn ’ s group showed a reversible mass gain and decline which are related to the oxygen uptake and release during cooling and heating, respectively [ 29 ]. The above works demonstrated that Mn vacancies are inclined to form during cooling process and then adjust the valence of Mn ions. Moreover, previous work has implied that a high-temperature quench ing treatment is possibly conducive to eliminate the Mn vacancies, which was reported by Komaba ’ s group designing distorted P2 0 -Na 0.67 MnO 2 with higher capacity than undistorted P2 - Na 0.67 MnO 2 [ 30 ], which represent a reasonable approach to synthesize a high-capacity Mn-based cathode material for NIBs [ 31–33 ]. However, such quenched samples still contain high content of Mn 3 þ ions entailing the notorious Jahn-Teller effect and thus multiphase transition during cycling and the enhanced long cycle performance of the material is missing in the work. Besides, a comprehensive study of the correlation between the cooling treatment and the structure of the material is also lacking. In this work, we propose a new approach to prepare Mn vacanciesfree Mn-based layered oxides cathode materials through a liquid N 2 lacking. In this work, we propose a new approach to prepare Mn vacanciesfree Mn-based layered oxides cathode materials through a liquid N 2 treatment. The rapid evaporation and extremely low temperatures (   196 � C) of liquid N 2 take the thermal energy away and freeze the material immediately, providing a superior quenching method to affect the inherent properties/structure of Mn-based layered oxides materials and further improve the specific capacity. Besides, an efficient strategy through low-cost Al and Fe co-doping is introduced to adjust the average valence of Mn ions and suppress biphasic structural transformations during Na þ (de)intercalation [ 33, 35 ]. The stronger Al - O bonding is beneficial to enlarging the spacing between the Na þ layers and stabilize the Na 0.67 MnO 2 structure as demonstrated in our previous work [ 35 ]. And a small amount of Fe-doping is confirmed to disrupt cations ordering and moderate the transition of P2 - O2 phases at high voltage, thus significantly enhancing cyclability of materials [ 33, 35 ]. Accord ingly, an optimized low-cost Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.9 O 2 material with both superior capacity and cycle performance is successfully prepared through this synergetic strategy including liquid N 2 quenching and aliovalent doping. Moreover, a systematic study on the correlation between the elec trochemical properties, long-range structure and local environment including the Mn ions average valence and structural distortion of the Na 0.67 M x Mn 1-x O 2 (M 1⁄4 Li þ, Mg 2 þ, Al 3 þ and Fe 3 þ ) materials via doping and different cooling processes (natural cooling, normal quenching and liquid N 2 quenching) is further understood by employing exand in-situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction (XRD), solid state nuclear magnetic reso nance (ss-NMR) and time-of-flight powder neutron diffraction (TOF PND). The results clearly reveal the superiority of the liquid N 2 quenching treatment, which can totally avoid the formation of TM va cancies and increase the atomic occupancy of Mn 4 þ /Mn 3 þ redox center, thus leading to the highest specific capacity, Na þ mobility and electronic conductivity among the prepared samples. Furthermore, the influence of doping on the Mn ions average valence, degree of structural distortion and electrochemical properties is discussed in depth. As a result, our work poses the most fertile directions in the development of high energy and stable cycling NIBs cathode materials based on sustainable Mn element, and provides an ultrahigh performance and low-cost cathode material for NIBs. 2. Experimental section 2.1. Synthesis of materials The Na 0.67 MnO 2, Na 0.67 M 0.1 Mn 0.9 O 2 (M 1⁄4 Li þ, Mg 2 þ, Al 3 þ ) and Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.85 O 2 samples were prepared via a simple citric acid-assisted sol-gel method. A stoichiometric amount of NaNO 3 (with 3% excess of sodium, AR, � 99%), Mn(CH 3 COO) 2 � 4H 2 O (AR, � 99%) and doped ions source were dissolved in 50 mL distilled water, and LiNO 3 (AR, � 99%), Mg(NO 3 ) 2 � 6H 2 O (AR, � 99%), Al(NO 3 ) 3 � 9H 2 O (AR, � 99%) and Fe(NO 3 ) 3 � 9H 2 O (AR, � 99%) were used as Li þ, Mg 2 þ, Al 3 þ and Fe 3 þ source, respectively. Then the mixed solution was added dropwise into a citric acid solution, and NH 3 ⋅ H 2 O (28%) was used to adjust the pH value to 7–8. The mixed solution was heated to 80 � C and stirred for 12 h to obtain a sol, then dried at 120 � C for 12 h to obtain a gel. The mixture was ground and heated at 450 � C for 6 h. After cooling down to room temperature, the powder was ground again and pressed into pellet, which was calcined at 900 � C for 15 h in air, and then sub jected to natural cooling (NC, average cooling rate: 0.8 � C min   1 ), quenching (QU, average cooling rate: 79.5 � C min   1 ) or liquid N 2 quenching (LN) to room temperature and finally stored in Ar-filled glove box. 2.2. Electrochemical tests � C min   1 ), quenching (QU, average cooling rate: 79.5 � C min   1 ) or liquid N 2 quenching (LN) to room temperature and finally stored in Ar-filled glove box. 2.2. Electrochemical tests Electrochemical experiments were carried out in 2032 coin-type cells. The working electrode was prepared by coating the slurry of the active materials, acetylene black, and polyvinylidene fluoride in the weight ratio of 8:1:1 using N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone as the solvent onto an Al foil substrate and dried overnight in a vacuum oven at 120 � C. The Na disks were made by rolling sodium chunks into thin sheets, which were then cut into circular plates and used as the counter electrode, and a glass fiber was used as the separator. The electrolyte was composed of a solution of 1 M NaClO 4 in propylene carbonate (PC) and fluoro ethylene carbonate (FEC) (95:5 by volume). The cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox. The cells were galvanostatically charged and discharged on a Neware CT-4008 battery test system at variable current X. Liu et al. Nano Energy 76 (2020) 104997 3 rates from 12 to 1200 mA g   1 (1200 mA g   1 1⁄4 5C). Rate capabilities tested by charging at 12 mA g   1 and discharging at the current densities ranging from 12 to 1200 mA g   1. For galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) analyses, the cells were cycled at 24 mA g   1. The duration time for each applied galvanostatic current and rest was 5 and 50 min, respectively. 2.3. Material characterizations Rigaku Ultima IV and SmartLab-SE X-ray diffractometer were used to collect powder XRD and variable temperature XRD patterns, respec tively, using Cu K α radiation ( λ 1⁄4 1.5406 Å) operated at 40 kV and 30 mA from 10 to 80 � (2 θ ) at a scan speed of 2 � min   1 or 10 � min   1. Timeof-flight powder neutron diffraction (TOF PND) was measured at the VULCAN instrument at the Spallation Neutron Sources (SNS), Oak Ridge National Laboratory (ORNL). The diffraction pattern was measured at the detector banks at 2 θ 1⁄4 � 90 �, equipped 5 mm receiving collimators. Neutron powder diffraction patterns were collected in the high intensity mode ( Δ d/d ~0.45%) for a duration of 2 h under the nominal 1.4 MW SNS operation, and then processed using VDRIVE software. Rietveld refinement of the XRD and ND patterns were performed using a General Structure Analysis System (GSAS) program [ 36 ]. In-situ synchrotron XRD patterns were collected in a transmission mode at room tempera ture, using ion chamber detectors at beamline BL14B1 of the Shanghai Synchrotron Radiation Facility (SSRF). For in-situ XRD, a standard CR2032 coin cell with small holes in each side was used to perform the experiment. Morphological features of the samples were obtained using a Hitachi S-4800 scanning electron microscope(SEM) and a Philips-FEI Tecnai F20 scanning transmission electron microscope (TEM), oper ating at 200 kV. The exact compositions of Na, Mn, Al and Fe were determined by Optima 2000-DV inductively coupled plasma emission spectrometry (ICP-OES, PerkinElmer). The ss-NMR experiments were performed on a Bruker AVANCE III 400 MHz NMR spectrometer. The 23 Na MAS NMR spectra were acquired using a 1.3 mm double-resonance HX probe at a spinning rate of 55 kHz with a Hahn-echo pulse sequence (90 � pulse - τ - 180 � pulse - τ ) or pj-MATPASS pulse sequence. The 90 � pulse length for 23 Na was 1.2 μ s and the recycle delay used was 20 ms. The 23 Na chemical shifts were referenced to 1 M NaCl aqueous solution (0 ppm). The spin-spin relaxation (T 2 ) were also measured by the Hahn-echo pulse sequence as a function of echo time. For direct-current polarization measurements, the fresh powders were compacted under 200 MPa pressure and subjected to various cooling treatments at room temperature. Both surface of pellets were polished and painted with aurum paste, and they were embedded in a stainless steel Swagelok cells to conduct electrical measurement [ 37 ]. The direct-current polarization measurements data were fitted by i 1⁄4 i ∞ þ ( i 0 i ∞ ) e -t/ τ. 3. Results and discussions 3.1. Characterization of prepared materials A group of Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.85 O 2 (NAFMO) materials were Fig. 1. Schematic illustration of naturally cooled, quenched and liquid N 2 quenched processes (left), and the effects of different cooling treatment on the vacancies of the electrode structures (right). X. Liu et al. Nano Energy 76 (2020) 104997 4 prepared by a sol-gel method followed by a cooling process. The syn thesis process is illustrated in Fig. 1. The precursor materials were sin tered at 900 � C for 15 h, and then exposed to three different cooling processes including natural cooling (NC), quenching (QU) and liquid N 2 (LN) quenching to 25 � C. Of note, liquid N 2 quenching method with rapid evaporation and extremely low temperatures (   196 � C) can freeze the samples immediately, providing much faster cooling rate and O 2 -free condition than normal quenching treatment. ICP-OES is employed to determine the stoichiometric ratios of NAFMO materials ( Table S1 ), which are consistent to the designed composition. The SEM images ( Figs. S1a - c ) are collected to reveal the morphologies of the prepared materials, exhibiting similar irregular polyhedron shape and smooth surface facets. Moreover, all of the prepared materials present a uniform distribution of Al, Fe and Mn elements and are shown in the TEM and EDS mapping results ( Fig. S1d -o). Nevertheless, NAFMO-NC, NAFMOQU and NAFMO-LN materials present the median particle size (D50) of 932.6, 756.5 and 742.3 nm ( Fig. S2 ), respectively, indicating the pri mary particle sizes decrease and the bulk diffusion length reduces with the increasing of cooling rate. Additionally, the XRD, TOF PND and ssNMR results demonstrate that the prepared NAFMO materials through different cooling process present very distinct atomic occupancy, local structure and even long-range structure. As shown in Fig. 2 a and S3, both of NAFMO-NC and NAFMO-QU materials exhibit a pure hexagonal layered crystalline structure of P2 phase with space group of P6 3 /mmc (PDF#27–0751), while the NAFMO-LN sample presents a distorted P2 phase (denoted as P2 ’, PDF#27–0752), which includes the standard diffraction patterns of P2 phase and four new diffraction peaks. These four peaks are attributed to the orthorhombic distortion of P2 phase and the decreasing symmetry of crystal lattice, generating diffraction peaks at the relatively lower diffraction angle in comparison to the (110), (112), (113) and (114) peaks from P2 phase. Due to the ordered arrangement of TM ions during natural cooling process, two honeycomb-superstructure peaks appear at ~22 � and ~32 � of NAFMOLN sample [ 16 ]. Here, in order to further describe the degree of distortion, the definition of δ is showed in Fig. S4, which is consistent with previous report [ 30 ]. The ratio of lattice constant of NAFMO-LN is calculated to be b ortho /a ortho 1⁄4 1.799, suggesting a slight distortion compared to b ortho /a ortho 1⁄4 1.830 of the P2 ’ phase Na 0.67 MnO 2 (S.G. Cmcm ), and the degree of distortion, δ, is estimated to be 2.9% (for the P2 ’ phase, δ 1⁄4 8%) [ 30 ]. The high-quality TOF PND data were further collected to investigate the existence of cations vacancies in the TM layers for its highly sensitivity to the distribution of atoms [ 38 ]. Rietveld refinements were performed with GSAS [ 36 ] and the results of the refined fractional atomic coordinates and atomic occupancy based on XRD and ND are presented in Table S2 and Table S3, respectively, which are highly consistent with each other and with low Rwp values. These results reveal that the atomic occupancies of TM ions in the NAFMO-NC, -QU and -LN samples are 0.922, 0.964 and 1, corresponding to the TM vacancies of 0.078, 0.035 and 0 in the TM layers ( Fig. 2 b), respectively. Moreover, the full atomic occupancy of Mn ions is expected to generate the lowest Mn ions average valence to satisfy the charge balance in NAFMO materials, which leads to the unequal Mn - O bonds and ortho rhombic distortion in the NAFMO-LN sample ( Fig. 1 and Table S4 ). Besides, the lattice parameters a, c and V increase continuously with the rising cooling rate ( Fig. S3 ), due to the larger ions radius of Mn 3 þ (0.645 Å) than Mn 4 þ Besides, the lattice parameters a, c and V increase continuously with the rising cooling rate ( Fig. S3 ), due to the larger ions radius of Mn 3 þ (0.645 Å) than Mn 4 þ (0.53 Å). More importantly, as shown in Table S3, the continuously expanded extent of Na þ layers spacing indicates that the faster cooling treatment would facilitate the Na þ insertion and extrac tion. In summary, our above results reveal that the long-range structure of materials is highly relevant to the cooling process, and a faster cooling treatment can suppress the formation of vacancy more effectively and thus increasing atomic occupancy of Mn 4 þ /Mn 3 þ redox center in the TM layers. To thoroughly study the structure of the prepared materials, ss-NMR spectroscopy was employed, which is highly sensitive to the local and electronic environment of target nucleus. Previous works have concluded that the characteristic signal of P2 and P2 ’ phase with obvious orthorhombic distortion are at around 1250 and 850 ppm, respectively [ 34, 39 ]. Surprisingly, as presented in Fig. 2 c and S5, the 23 Na NMR spectra of the three materials only show one resonance signal at 1200–1450 ppm, even for the P2 ’ phase NAFMO-LN sample confirmed by XRD and TOF PND results. The results indicate that such a slight orthorhombic distortion of NAFMO-LN sample have no detectable effect on the local electronic environment of Na þ. Besides, all the spectra show a signal at around 0 ppm due to the diamagnetic sodium salts. Despite the chemical shifts and peak patterns for these three samples are Fig. 2. Structural characterization of Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.85 O 2. (a) Powder XRD patterns, (b) the atomic occupancy fitting results of TM ions from TOF PND data, and (c) 23 Na ss-NMR spectra of the prepared materials and the asterisks (*) indicate the spinning sidebands. (d - f) Rietveld refinements of TOF PND patterns for the asprepared samples. X. Liu et al. Nano Energy 76 (2020) 104997 5 similar, the full width at half maximum for NAFMO-NC (97.49 ppm) sample is significantly broader than that for NAFMO-QU (64.99 ppm) and NAFMO-LN (64.88 ppm) samples ( Fig. S5 ), which indicates that the uniformity of the Na þ local environment improves when accelerating the cooling rate. The local environment of Na þ is highly affected by the number of surrounding Mn ions though Na - O - Mn pathways and the presence of TM ions vacancies increases the types of local environment. Moreover, a higher content of paramagnetic Mn ions will cause a stronger Fermi-contact interaction around Na þ and thus a large chemi cal shift [ 40 ]. The resonance of NAFMO-QU sample locates at a larger chemical shift than that of the NAFMO-NC sample ( Fig. 2 c), which further confirm Mn atomic occupancy of NAFMO-QU is higher than that of NAFMO-NC sample. Additionally, the resonance shifts slightly to a lower chemical shift for the NAFMO-LN sample than NAFMO-QU sam ple, owing to the much longer Mn - O bonds in orthorhombic distortion phase ( Fig. 1 and Table S4 ) and consequently the Fermi-contact inter action around Na þ decreased. To further reveal the effects of different cooling rates on the Na þ (de) intercalation, dynamics of NAFMO-NC/QU/LN materials, T 2 (spin spin relaxation times) were obtained by the Hahn-echo method and the fitted results are showed in Fig. 3 a. For the paramagnetic solid-state materials, T 2 can eliminate the influence of transition metal ions and the faster Na þ hopping rate leads to longer T 2 value [ 41, 42 ]. As shown in Fig. 3 b, the T 2 value continuously increases with rising the cooling rate and reaches the highest value of 103 μ s for the NAFMO-LN sample, which is assigned to the largest Na þ layers spacing ( Table S4 ) and the fastest Na þ hopping rate. Besides, the electronic conductivity, as another important param eter for evaluating electrode materials, was measured by direct-current polarization method, and the results are showed in Fig. 3 c - d. The ob tained electronic conductivity of 3.812 x 10   5 S cm   1 for NAFMO-LN sample is higher than the NAFMO-QU sample (3.227 x 10   5 S cm   1 ) and much higher than NAFMO-NC sample (9.790 x 10   6 S cm   1 ), which may be attributed to the increasing contents of Mn 3 þ promoting electron hopping [ 37 ]. Thus, an excellent rate performance of NAFMO-LN sam ple is expected, due to the fastest Na þ mobility and highest electronic conductivity. The above results have shown that the long-range and local structure of materials are closely related to the cooling rate during the synthesis process. Next, the XRD patterns at variable temperature were performed for a better understanding of the structure evolution during cooling process. Specifically, the precursor was firstly heated up to 900 � C for 15 h in air, and then slowly cooled down to each temperature point. As shown in Fig. S6, the XRD patterns reveal that the material at 900 � C matches well with the standard patterns of P2 ’ phase, attributed to the low average valence of Mn ions thus leading to an orthorhombic distortion of P2 phase as commented before [ 29 ]. As the temperature decreases, the characteristic diffraction peaks (marked with “ y “ ) of P2 ’ phase are reduced in intensity and they are consecutively shifted to slightly higher diffraction angles due to the contraction of ab plane. The fact corresponds the disappearance of the orthorhombic distortion and the increasing of Mn ions average valence. As the temperature is cooled down to 800 � C and beneath, only P2 phase is observed and its diffraction signals fit perfectly the standard patterns of P2 phase, probably due to the fact that the crystal lattice tends to uptake oxygen in the cooling process [ 29 ]. The XRD data obtained at variable temperature fully reveal that the structure of the material subjected at different the cooling process [ 29 ]. The XRD data obtained at variable temperature fully reveal that the structure of the material subjected at different temperature during cooling process could be obtained by appropriately controlling the cooling rate, and the faster cooling treatment promote maintaining the structure of high-temperature (900 � C). 3.2. Electrochemical performance of Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.85 O 2 cathode materials The electrochemical performance of the NAFMO-NC/QU/LN Fig. 3. (a - b) 23 Na NMR T 2 fitting results and (c - d) direct-current polarization fitting results of prepared materials. Electronic conductivity is calculated by i ∞ and Ohm ’ s law. X. Liu et al. Nano Energy 76 (2020) 104997 6 materials was evaluated in Na half cells at 30 � C. The galvanostatic charge-discharge curves of the electrodes within the voltage range of 2.0–4.0 V at 12 mA g   1 (0.05C) current rate are shown in Fig. 4 a. As the cooling rate for the prepared materials increases, the following charac teristics of first cycle curves deserve special attention: (i) These three samples exhibit smooth charge-discharge profiles, confirming multiple phase transition and Na þ -vacancy ordering during (de)intercalation processes have been suppressed by the introduction of Al and Fe ele ments. While the plateaus at ~2.2 V and ~3.5 V become more obvious with increase of cooling rate, which are related to phase transition processes and will be discussed in the next section. (ii) The open circuit voltages ( Fig. S7 ) of the electrodes decrease continuously with the increasing of cooling rate, corresponding to the decrease of Mn ions average valence. (iii) The initial discharge capacities are 131, 175 and 202 mAh g   1 for NAFMO-NC, NAFMO-QU and NAFMO-LN, respec tively. They are significantly improved with the accelerating cooling rate. To the best our knowledge, NAFMO-LN material has shown the highest initial discharge capacity and specific energy density (~527 Wh kg   1, solely considering the mass of cathode active material) among reported P2-type sodium oxides materials in the voltage range of 2.0–4.0 V. Furthermore, as the working window is expanded to 1.5–4.5 V ( Fig. S8 ), the capacities delivered by NAFMO-NC, NAFMO-QU and NAFMO-LN are 210, 224 and 246 mA g   1, respectively, while an obvious plateau attributing to anionic redox reaction appears at ~4.2 V [ 17, 43, 44 ]. As indicated above, the anionic charge compensation pro cess usually shows the poor cyclabilities and slow dynamics in both sodium cathode materials and Li-analogues, so we mainly focus on the working potential below 4 V. In order to meet the needs of the market of smart grids, the requirement for the discharge rates of NIBs is much higher. The rate capabilities were tested by charging at low current and discharging at various currents, the same as previous researches [ 33, 35, 39 ], and the results are shown in Fig. 4 b - c. NAFMO-LN electrode shows the highest capacities and stable cyclabilities at each current density, providing the capacities of 202, 191, 173, 161 and 148 mAh g   1 as the discharge current densities are increased from 12, 24, 120, 240–480 mA g   1, respectively. Even with the 100 times increase of the current den sity (1200 mA g   1 ), the capacity retention is up to 60% (122 mAh g   1 ), indicating excellent rate capability among the reported high-rate P2-type electrodes. The diffusion coefficients of Na þ were calculated from GITT results to evaluate the (de)intercalation of kinetics of Na þ during first cycle. As shown in Fig. S9, the geomean D Na þ of NAFMO-LN (2.474 � 10   10 cm² s   1 ) is slight higher than NAFMO-QU (2.196 � 10   10 cm² s   1 ) and is much higher than that of NAFMO-NC (5.107 � 10   11 cm² s   1 ), which is consistent with the best rate performance of liquid N 2 treatment sample. In addition, the cycle stabilities of the ma terials are also investigated at different current densities of 12 and 1200 mA g   1 as shown in Fig. S10 and 4d, respectively. At the current density of 12 mA g   1, NAFMO-LN electrode delivers the highest initial discharge capacity of 202 mAh g   1 and the highest maintained capacity of 198 mAh g   1 after ten cycles without any electrode modifications. Espe cially, the excellent reversibility and negligible voltage change are found in the charge-discharge curves of NAFMO-LN electrodes. Due to the excellent rate performance of NAFMO-LN, this electrode was subjected to further galvanostatic charged-discharged cycles at such a high current rate of 1200 mA g   1 excellent rate performance of NAFMO-LN, this electrode was subjected to further galvanostatic charged-discharged cycles at such a high current rate of 1200 mA g   1. As shown in Fig. 4 d and S11, it also maintains the initial capacity of 122 mAh g   1 and the long cycling stability is achieved with the remarkable capacity retentions of 98% after 100 cycles, 95% after 200 cycles and 81% after 600 cycles at 1200 mA g   1 with ~100% coulombic efficiency during cycling. The plateaus of ~2.2 V and ~3.5 V are substantial reduction from the comparison dQ/dV curves at low (12 mA g   1 ) and high (1200 mA g   1 ) current densities, especially the plateau at low voltage which is deemed as the main reason for the ca pacity decays due to the notorious Mn 3 þ Jahn-Teller effects. Ex-situ 23 Na NMR was further performed to explore the structure evolutions at high current density. As shown in Fig. S12, the P2 phase signal is maintained during the whole charge process, and thus the electrode could exhibit Fig. 4. Long-range and local structural transformations during (De) Sodiation processes In-situ synchrotron XRD was employed to track the long-range structural transformations of NAFMO-LN electrode induced by the (de)intercalation of Na þ at a rate current of 24 mA g   1. As shown in Fig. 5 a, the XRD patterns have been divided into four stages to clarify the structure evolution process more clearly. The (002), (110), (112), (113) and (114) peaks are retained during the whole process, corresponding to a quasi-solid-solution reaction rather than typically multiple-phases transition reaction according to the previous reports [ 45,46 ]. As indi cated above, the prepared electrode is assigned to a P2 0 I phase, which exhibits a slight orthorhombic distortion from P2 phase. At the begin ning of charge process, the (020) peak reduces in intensity and shifts to higher angle, corresponding to the decrease of distortion degree, and a P2 phase is formed at x 1⁄4 0.40. Afterwards, a board peak is observed at the higher diffraction angle of 9 � at the end of charge process, owing to the formation of small amount of O-type stacking in the P2 structure. As reported by Bruce ’ s group, this process should be indicated as a “ Z ” phase evolved by the intergrowth mechanism, which have been mentioned in many P2 compounds, especially those containing Fe 3 þ [ 35, 47, 48 ]. The formation of “ Z ” phase is more moderate than O2 phase at high voltage, as the lattice parameter changes are smaller in magni tude and less abrupt, leading to a stable structure and better capacity retention [ 49 ]. The structural evolution is highly reversible during discharge. Of note, the (002), (110), (112), (113) and (114) peaks of P2 phase maintain in the rest of the discharge process, rather than replaced by that of typical P2 ’ phase with high degree of orthorhombic distortion (denoted as P2 ’ II). As the x equals 0.79, the characteristic signals of P2 ’ II phase are presented in both in-situ XRD and ss-NMR ( Fig. 5 b) results. However, such a moderate phase transition also makes it hard to distinguish between the P2 ’ I and P2 ’ II phase from in-situ XRD results. As shown in Fig. 5 b, the local environment information of the elec trode materials can be observed through 23 Na MAS NMR spectra, since Na þ directly participates in the electrochemical reaction in NIBs. 23 Na 1D slices were extracted from²D projection-MATPASS (pj-MATPASS) NMR spectra to eliminate the sideband signals [ 50 ]. Consistent with the powder sample, the ss-NMR spectrum of NAFMO-LN pristine electrode also shows the resonance signals at ~1250 ppm and ~0 ppm, rather than monoclinic and hydrated phases that usually occur during elec trode prepared process, suggesting that P2 ’ I phase is stable and structural transitions do not occur during electrode preparing procedure [ 34, 51 ]. During initial charge and discharge processes, the chemical shift of the signal moves to the high and low value gradually, corre spongding to the increase and decrease of the Mn ions average valence, respectively. The local structural evolution is in good agreement with the results of in situ XRD. When the x 1⁄4 0.32, the amount of local environment significantly increases because of the glide of MnO 2 layers, a wide peak at ~1100 ppm emerges, corresponding to the “ Z ” phase. Besides, the P2 phase signal can also be observed at the high voltage and decrease in intensity with “ Z ” phase emerging, which further proves the formation of “ Z ” phase is an intermediate and moderate process evolving intergrowth of P2 and O2 phases. The local structure is also highly reversible in discharge process. In x 1⁄4 0.78, the characteristic signal of P2 ’ II phase appears at ~850 ppm, which are clearly distin guished from the signal of P2 ’ I phase. Besides, the P2 ’ II phase signal signal of P2 ’ II phase appears at ~850 ppm, which are clearly distin guished from the signal of P2 ’ I phase. Besides, the P2 ’ II phase signal of NAFMO-LN sample is much broader than that of non-doped P2 0 -Na 0.67 MnO 2 materials while similar to the stable P2 - Na 0.67 Mg 0.1 Mn 0.9 O 2 and P2 - Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Mn 0.9 O 2 materials in previ ous works [ 34, 39 ], corresponding to a much more moderate structure transition and thus better cycle performance. 3.4. Effects of various doping elements on structural and electrochemical properties of materials In order to give some illustration on the significance of the work, the Mn ions average valence of layered oxide materias have been adjusted by introducting different valence doping element, e.g. the introduction of Li þ is expected to rise 3 folds molar equivalents of Mn 3 þ to Mn 4 þ, and 2 folds for Mg 2 þ and equal amount for Al 3 þ. Therefore, Na 0.67 M 0.1 Mn 0.9 O 2 (M 1⁄4 Li þ, Mg 2 þ, Al 3 þ ) and non-doped materials were also prepared via the same synthesis process to further reveal the rela tionship between the Mn oxidation state, structure and electrochemical properties. The longand short-range structural information are showed in Fig. 6. The obvious orthorhombic distortion of P2 phase can been found in non-doped NMO-QU/-LN and Al 3 þ -doped NAMO-LN samples with exhibiting the characteristic signals of P2 ’ phase in both XRD patterns and ss-NMR spectra. Also, orthorhombic distortion is observed for Mg 2 þ -doped NMMO-LN sample, just like the above results of NAFMO-LN sample. While the Li þ -doped NLMO samples do not show any distortion in both longand short-structure by each cooling treat ment. Besides, the full width at half maximum of P2 phase also become narrow continuously with the faster cooling treatment for each prepared material. The above results indicate that the orthorhombic distortion tends to exist in the materials with low theoretical manganese valence and TM vacancies can be suppressed for P2 type Na 0.67 M x Mn 1-x O 2 by the faster cooling treatment, and then exhibiting a more uniform local environment. A comparison of the electrochemical performance of non-doped and doped Na 0.67 M x Mn 1-x O 2 materials (Li þ, Mg 2 þ, Al 3 þ and Fe 3 þ ) prepared by NC, QU and LN methods is shown in Fig. 6 a - e. The initial charge and discharge capacities of all prepared materials increase significantly with the accelerating cooling rate, and the maximum capacity is obtained by liquid N 2 quenching (LN) treatment for all samples, demonstrating that the rapid cooling treatment can eliminate Mn vacancies, increase redox centers, and effectively improve the capacity of P2 type Na 0.67 M x Mn 1x O 2. Moreover, Fig. 7 f and S13 show that the cycle stablity of Na 0.67 M x Mn 1-x O 2 materials is highly related to the Mn ions average valence in the materials except for NAFMO-LN sample, which exhibits optimal electrochemical performance through the synergy effect of Al and Fe co-doping [ 32, 33 ]. The lower Mn ions average valence causes more severe orthorhombic distortion in Na 0.67 M x Mn 1-x O 2 and thus being detrimental to cyclability, e.g. the highest specific capacity (215 mAh g   1, 2–4 V) but lowest capacity retention of non-doped NMO-LN (Mn þ 3.33, δ 1⁄4 5.59%) sample, exhibiting many small plateau in galva nostatic charge-discharge curves. In contrast, the doped samples exhibit much smoother charge-discharge profiles, which evidences that the introduction of non-electrochemically active element disrupt the TM ordering in the TM layers and hamper Na þ -vacancy ordering during cycling [ 13 ]. The 10 mol% of Li-doping material presents a pure P2 phase without any lattice distortion and a stable cycling performance. However, even with liquid N 2 quenched treatment, NLMO-LN (Mn þ 3.59, δ 1⁄4 0%) sample only delivers specific capacity of 130 mAh g   1. There However, even with liquid N 2 quenched treatment, NLMO-LN (Mn þ 3.59, δ 1⁄4 0%) sample only delivers specific capacity of 130 mAh g   1. There fore, a rational element substitution should be implemented to mitigate Jahn-Teller effect and make a balance bewteen the capacity and cycle stablity to achieve superior electrochemical performance. Thus, the optimal co-doped NAFMO-LN material, with moderate initial Mn ions valence (Mn þ 3.39 ) and slight distortion ( δ 1⁄4 2.91%), shows not only an outstanding capacity of 202 mAh g   1 at voltage range of 2–4 V, but also a satifactory cyclability of 90.4% after 30 cycles at 12 mA g   1. 4. Conclusion In summary, our comparative results obtained in Na cells of nondoped and doped Na 0.67 M x Mn 1-x O 2 materials with Li þ, Mg 2 þ, Al 3 þ and Fe 3 þ prepared via different cooling processes is verified by using the combination of in-situ synchrotron XRD, TOF PND and ss-NMR mea surements. They reveal that a simple liquid N 2 quenching treatment X. Liu et al. Nano Energy 76 (2020) 104997 9 Fig. 6. (a - d) Powder XRD and (e - h) ss-NMR spectra of Li þ -doped, Mg 2 þ -doped, Al 3 þ -doped and non-doped Na 0.67 MnO 2 materials with different cooling treatment. Na 0.67 Li 0.1 Mn 0.9 O 2, Na 0.67 Mg 0.1 Mn 0.9 O 2, Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Mn 0.9 O 2 and Na 0.67 MnO 2 are denoted as NLMO, NMMO, NAMO and NMO, respectively. The spinning sideband signal and the characteristic signal of orthorhombic distortion are marked with “ * “ and ” z “, respectively. X. Liu et al. Nano Energy 76 (2020) 104997 10 could be regarded as a genaral synergetic strategy to control the for mation of vacancy in TM layers and thus to enhance Mn 4 þ /Mn 3 þ redox center. Consequently, the deliverable capacity of all the Mn-based Na 0.67 M x Mn 1-x O 2 materials can be signifcantly improved below 4.0 V, eliminating the factors responsible of capacity dacay such as electrolyte decomposition, structural collapse and oxygen loss at high voltgate. The ss-NMR, GITT and direct-current polarization results demonstrate the highest Na þ mobility and electronic conductivity for the liquid N 2 quenching sample, among the three different cooling rate samples. Moreover, we introduced Al and Fe, the top two earth abundance metal elements into TM layers to form the cost-effective Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.85 O 2 compound. After liquid N 2 quenching, the slightly dis torted Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.85 O 2 delivers a remarkable capacity of 202 and 246 mAh g   1 within 2.0–4.0 V and 1.5–4.5 V, respectively. Even with 100 times higher current density (1200 mA g   1 ), the specific capacity remains up to 122 mAh g   1 over 600 cycles with 81% capacity retention, indicating exceptional rate and power capabilities with excellent cycling stability. The outstanding overall performance of Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.2 material demonstrated the synergetic strategy of liquid N 2 quenching treatment and rational element substitution, which can be used as a general guide for the further development of high ca pacity, high power and stable cycling materials, especially for Mn-based layered electrodes in NIBs. Al and Fe-containing Mn-based layered cathode with controlled vacancies for high-rate sodium ion batteries Xiangsi Liu a, Guiming Zhong c, Zhumei Xiao a, Bizhu Zheng a, Wenhua Zuo a, Ke Zhou a, Haodong Liu d, Ziteng Liang a, Yuxuan Xiang a, Zirong Chen a, Gregorio F. Ortiz a, e, Riqiang Fu f, Yong Yang a, b, * a State Key Laboratory for Physical Chemistry of Solid Surfaces, And Department of Chemistry, College of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Xiamen University, Xiamen, 361005, People ’ s Republic of China b School of Energy Research, Xiamen University, Xiamen, 361005, People ’ s Republic of China c Xiamen Institute of Rare Earth Materials, And Fujian Institute of Research on the Structure of Matter, Haixi Institutes, Chinese Academy of Sciences, Fujian, 361021, People ’ s Republic of China d Department of Nanoengineering, University of California San Diego, La Jolla, CA, 92093, United States e Departamento de Química Inorg �anica e Ingeniería Química, Instituto Universitario de Investigaci �on en Química Fina y Nanoquímica (IUNAN), Universidad de C �ordoba, Campus de Rabanales, Edificio Marie Curie, E-14071, C �ordoba, Spain f National High Magnetic Field Laboratory, 1800 E. Paul Dirac Drive, Tallahassee, FL, 32310, United States ARTICLE INFO Keywords: Sodium-ion batteries Na 0.67 MnO 2 Liquid N 2 quenching Manganese vacancies Solid-state NMR spectroscopy ABSTRACT Mn-based layered oxides as one of the most promising and cost-effective cathode candidates for sodium-ion batteries still face great challenge to achieve high capacity with long cycle life under high-rate current simul taneously. In this work, we propose an effective strategy by a combination of liquid N 2 quenching and aliovalent doping to get new layered cathode materials. As evidenced by in-situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction, time-of-flight powder neutron diffraction and solid-state 23 Na nuclear magnetic resonance techniques, the proposed synthesis methods allow tuning the transition metal ions vacancies and enhance Mn 4 þ /Mn 3 þ redox center of P2-type Mnbased materials. Our results demonstrate that such an optimized structure significantly enhances the deliverable capacity, Na þ mobility and electronic conductivity of the materials. Furthermore, the effects of aliovalent doping elements and different cooling approaches on the long-range structure, local environment and electrochemical performance are comprehensively studied by comparing a wide range of doped Na 0.67 M x Mn 1-x O 2 (M 1⁄4 Li, Mg, Al, Fe) materials. The optimized Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.85 O 2 material exhibits a remarkably high initial capacity of 202 mAh g   1 among ever reported P2-type layered oxides within 2–4 V, a stable capacity retention of 81% after 600 cycles and outstanding rate capability of the specific capacity up to 122 mAh g   1 at 1200 mA g   1. 1. Introduction The rapid development of intelligent and environmental-friendly industrial technology, including smart mobile phone and green elec tric vehicles, has made rechargeable batteries an indispensable part of our modern society [ 1, 2 ]. Among various energy storage systems, lithium ion batteries (LIBs) with superior high energy and power den sities performance have accounted for the key position [ 3 ]. Neverthe less, the low reserve and uneven distribution of Li resources, and the use of high-cost and toxicity of Co and Ni as redox center for mostly LIBs cathode materials fail to fulfill demands of sustainable development [ 4 ]. Recently, sodium ion batteries (NIBs) have attracted many interests and are considered as the most promising candidate of LIBs, due to the fact that sodium possesses similar chemical properties and higher natural abundance than lithium [ 5, 6 ]. are considered as the most promising candidate of LIBs, due to the fact that sodium possesses similar chemical properties and higher natural abundance than lithium [ 5, 6 ]. In the development of NIBs, cathode is the most critical component that restricts cost and energy density in the full batteries [ 7, 8 ]. Comparing to polyanionic and tunnel-type cathodes materials for NIBs, layered transition metal oxides (Na x MO 2, M 1⁄4 transition metal) are extensively studied because of their high theoretical specific capacity * Corresponding author. State Key Laboratory for Physical Chemistry of Solid Surfaces, and Department of Chemistry, College of Chemistry and Chemical Engi neering, Xiamen University, Xiamen, 361005, People ’ s Republic of China. E-mail address: yyang@xmu.edu.cn (Y. Yang). Contents lists available at ScienceDirect Nano Energy journal homepage: http://www. elsevier.co m/locate/n anoen https://doi.org/10.1016/j.nanoen.2020.104997 Received 22 March 2020; Received in revised form 16 May 2020; Accepted 16 May 2020 Nano Energy 76 (2020) 104997 2 and fast Na þ mobility [ 9 ]. Among them, the low-cost Mn-based layered materials are deemed as ideal cathodes for large-scale energy storage. Two types of stacking structures, O3(ABCABC stacking) and P2(AABB stacking) are frequently observed in most of as-synthesized Na x MnO 2 materials within layered structure materials [ 10 ]. In comparison to the (de)sodiation of O3-type materials via the interstitial tetrahedral sites with relatively higher hopping energy from one octahedral site to another, the P2-type phase allows a direct migration between adjacent prismatic sites, thus enabling rapid Na þ transport and showing better applicability [ 11 ]. However, the available capacities of P2-type cathode materials are still insufficient yet [ 12, 13 ]. To address this issue, anionic redox reaction is introduced as its Li counterpart, which can be triggered to get extra capacity by charging the cells to a higher voltage range of 4.0–4.5 V vs. Na þ /Na (4.3–4.8 V vs. Li þ /Li). Unfortunately, the cathode materials inevitably suffer from electrolyte decomposition, multiple phase transition and even oxygen loss at such a high voltage, resulting in obvious voltage hysteresis and rapid capacity fade [ 14–17 ]. In contrast, controlling the cut-off voltage lower than 4 V for P2-type cathode can effectively eliminate aforementioned shortages and yield a much better cyclability, but the reversible capacity is hardly higher than 175 mAh g   1 [ 18–22 ]. Recent investigations have focused on the optimization of the crys talline/local structures and morphology engineering of Na x MnO 2 by adjusting the Na/Mn ratio, sintering temperature, cooling rate and aliovalent doping [ 20–25 ], which further determine the electrochemical performance. However, the overall performance of the materials is far below our expectation. The understanding of underlying structure-property relationship of the materials, especially the local environment including Mn vacancies, is insufficient for obtaining the material with high capacity and long cycle performance. The formation of Mn vacancies in the transition metal (TM) layers have been observed over 40 years [ 26–29 ]. Parant et al. proposed that Na 0.67 MnO 2 is able to uptake oxygen during the cooling process [ 27 ], resulting in the lift of Mn ions oxidation state and the formation of manganese deficient phases (denoted as □ ): Na 0.67 MnO 2 þ y/2 O 2 → Na 0.67 MnO 2 þ y (Na 0.67/(1 þ y/2) Mn 1/(1 þ y/2) □ (y/2)/(1 þ y/ 2) O 2 ) The thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) results of Dahn ’ s group showed a reversible mass gain and decline which are related to the oxygen uptake and release during cooling and heating, respectively [ 29 ]. The above works demonstrated that Mn vacancies are inclined to form during cooling process and then adjust the valence of Mn ions. Moreover, previous work has implied that a high-temperature quench ing treatment is possibly conducive to eliminate the Mn vacancies, which was reported by Komaba ’ s group designing distorted P2 0 -Na 0.67 MnO 2 with higher capacity than undistorted P2 - Na 0.67 MnO 2 [ 30 ], which represent a reasonable approach to synthesize a high-capacity Mn-based cathode material for NIBs [ 31–33 ]. However, such quenched samples still contain high content of Mn 3 þ ions entailing the notorious Jahn-Teller effect and thus multiphase transition during cycling and the enhanced long cycle performance of the material is missing in the work. Besides, a comprehensive study of the correlation between the cooling treatment and the structure of the material is also lacking. In this work, we propose a new approach to prepare Mn vacanciesfree Mn-based layered oxides cathode materials through a liquid N 2 lacking. In this work, we propose a new approach to prepare Mn vacanciesfree Mn-based layered oxides cathode materials through a liquid N 2 treatment. The rapid evaporation and extremely low temperatures (   196 � C) of liquid N 2 take the thermal energy away and freeze the material immediately, providing a superior quenching method to affect the inherent properties/structure of Mn-based layered oxides materials and further improve the specific capacity. Besides, an efficient strategy through low-cost Al and Fe co-doping is introduced to adjust the average valence of Mn ions and suppress biphasic structural transformations during Na þ (de)intercalation [ 33, 35 ]. The stronger Al - O bonding is beneficial to enlarging the spacing between the Na þ layers and stabilize the Na 0.67 MnO 2 structure as demonstrated in our previous work [ 35 ]. And a small amount of Fe-doping is confirmed to disrupt cations ordering and moderate the transition of P2 - O2 phases at high voltage, thus significantly enhancing cyclability of materials [ 33, 35 ]. Accord ingly, an optimized low-cost Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.9 O 2 material with both superior capacity and cycle performance is successfully prepared through this synergetic strategy including liquid N 2 quenching and aliovalent doping. Moreover, a systematic study on the correlation between the elec trochemical properties, long-range structure and local environment including the Mn ions average valence and structural distortion of the Na 0.67 M x Mn 1-x O 2 (M 1⁄4 Li þ, Mg 2 þ, Al 3 þ and Fe 3 þ ) materials via doping and different cooling processes (natural cooling, normal quenching and liquid N 2 quenching) is further understood by employing exand in-situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction (XRD), solid state nuclear magnetic reso nance (ss-NMR) and time-of-flight powder neutron diffraction (TOF PND). The results clearly reveal the superiority of the liquid N 2 quenching treatment, which can totally avoid the formation of TM va cancies and increase the atomic occupancy of Mn 4 þ /Mn 3 þ redox center, thus leading to the highest specific capacity, Na þ mobility and electronic conductivity among the prepared samples. Furthermore, the influence of doping on the Mn ions average valence, degree of structural distortion and electrochemical properties is discussed in depth. As a result, our work poses the most fertile directions in the development of high energy and stable cycling NIBs cathode materials based on sustainable Mn element, and provides an ultrahigh performance and low-cost cathode material for NIBs. 2. Experimental section 2.1. Synthesis of materials The Na 0.67 MnO 2, Na 0.67 M 0.1 Mn 0.9 O 2 (M 1⁄4 Li þ, Mg 2 þ, Al 3 þ ) and Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.85 O 2 samples were prepared via a simple citric acid-assisted sol-gel method. A stoichiometric amount of NaNO 3 (with 3% excess of sodium, AR, � 99%), Mn(CH 3 COO) 2 � 4H 2 O (AR, � 99%) and doped ions source were dissolved in 50 mL distilled water, and LiNO 3 (AR, � 99%), Mg(NO 3 ) 2 � 6H 2 O (AR, � 99%), Al(NO 3 ) 3 � 9H 2 O (AR, � 99%) and Fe(NO 3 ) 3 � 9H 2 O (AR, � 99%) were used as Li þ, Mg 2 þ, Al 3 þ and Fe 3 þ source, respectively. Then the mixed solution was added dropwise into a citric acid solution, and NH 3 ⋅ H 2 O (28%) was used to adjust the pH value to 7–8. The mixed solution was heated to 80 � C and stirred for 12 h to obtain a sol, then dried at 120 � C for 12 h to obtain a gel. The mixture was ground and heated at 450 � C for 6 h. After cooling down to room temperature, the powder was ground again and pressed into pellet, which was calcined at 900 � C for 15 h in air, and then sub jected to natural cooling (NC, average cooling rate: 0.8 � C min   1 ), quenching (QU, average cooling rate: 79.5 � C min   1 ) or liquid N 2 quenching (LN) to room temperature and finally stored in Ar-filled glove box. 2.2. Electrochemical tests � C min   1 ), quenching (QU, average cooling rate: 79.5 � C min   1 ) or liquid N 2 quenching (LN) to room temperature and finally stored in Ar-filled glove box. 2.2. Electrochemical tests Electrochemical experiments were carried out in 2032 coin-type cells. The working electrode was prepared by coating the slurry of the active materials, acetylene black, and polyvinylidene fluoride in the weight ratio of 8:1:1 using N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone as the solvent onto an Al foil substrate and dried overnight in a vacuum oven at 120 � C. The Na disks were made by rolling sodium chunks into thin sheets, which were then cut into circular plates and used as the counter electrode, and a glass fiber was used as the separator. The electrolyte was composed of a solution of 1 M NaClO 4 in propylene carbonate (PC) and fluoro ethylene carbonate (FEC) (95:5 by volume). The cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox. The cells were galvanostatically charged and discharged on a Neware CT-4008 battery test system at variable current X. Liu et al. Nano Energy 76 (2020) 104997 3 rates from 12 to 1200 mA g   1 (1200 mA g   1 1⁄4 5C). Rate capabilities tested by charging at 12 mA g   1 and discharging at the current densities ranging from 12 to 1200 mA g   1. For galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) analyses, the cells were cycled at 24 mA g   1. The duration time for each applied galvanostatic current and rest was 5 and 50 min, respectively. 2.3. Material characterizations Rigaku Ultima IV and SmartLab-SE X-ray diffractometer were used to collect powder XRD and variable temperature XRD patterns, respec tively, using Cu K α radiation ( λ 1⁄4 1.5406 Å) operated at 40 kV and 30 mA from 10 to 80 � (2 θ ) at a scan speed of 2 � min   1 or 10 � min   1. Timeof-flight powder neutron diffraction (TOF PND) was measured at the VULCAN instrument at the Spallation Neutron Sources (SNS), Oak Ridge National Laboratory (ORNL). The diffraction pattern was measured at the detector banks at 2 θ 1⁄4 � 90 �, equipped 5 mm receiving collimators. Neutron powder diffraction patterns were collected in the high intensity mode ( Δ d/d ~0.45%) for a duration of 2 h under the nominal 1.4 MW SNS operation, and then processed using VDRIVE software. Rietveld refinement of the XRD and ND patterns were performed using a General Structure Analysis System (GSAS) program [ 36 ]. In-situ synchrotron XRD patterns were collected in a transmission mode at room tempera ture, using ion chamber detectors at beamline BL14B1 of the Shanghai Synchrotron Radiation Facility (SSRF). For in-situ XRD, a standard CR2032 coin cell with small holes in each side was used to perform the experiment. Morphological features of the samples were obtained using a Hitachi S-4800 scanning electron microscope(SEM) and a Philips-FEI Tecnai F20 scanning transmission electron microscope (TEM), oper ating at 200 kV. The exact compositions of Na, Mn, Al and Fe were determined by Optima 2000-DV inductively coupled plasma emission spectrometry (ICP-OES, PerkinElmer). The ss-NMR experiments were performed on a Bruker AVANCE III 400 MHz NMR spectrometer. The 23 Na MAS NMR spectra were acquired using a 1.3 mm double-resonance HX probe at a spinning rate of 55 kHz with a Hahn-echo pulse sequence (90 � pulse - τ - 180 � pulse - τ ) or pj-MATPASS pulse sequence. The 90 � pulse length for 23 Na was 1.2 μ s and the recycle delay used was 20 ms. The 23 Na chemical shifts were referenced to 1 M NaCl aqueous solution (0 ppm). The spin-spin relaxation (T 2 ) were also measured by the Hahn-echo pulse sequence as a function of echo time. For direct-current polarization measurements, the fresh powders were compacted under 200 MPa pressure and subjected to various cooling treatments at room temperature. Both surface of pellets were polished and painted with aurum paste, and they were embedded in a stainless steel Swagelok cells to conduct electrical measurement [ 37 ]. The direct-current polarization measurements data were fitted by i 1⁄4 i ∞ þ ( i 0 i ∞ ) e -t/ τ. 3. Results and discussions 3.1. Characterization of prepared materials A group of Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.85 O 2 (NAFMO) materials were Fig. 1. Schematic illustration of naturally cooled, quenched and liquid N 2 quenched processes (left), and the effects of different cooling treatment on the vacancies of the electrode structures (right). X. Liu et al. Nano Energy 76 (2020) 104997 4 prepared by a sol-gel method followed by a cooling process. The syn thesis process is illustrated in Fig. 1. The precursor materials were sin tered at 900 � C for 15 h, and then exposed to three different cooling processes including natural cooling (NC), quenching (QU) and liquid N 2 (LN) quenching to 25 � C. Of note, liquid N 2 quenching method with rapid evaporation and extremely low temperatures (   196 � C) can freeze the samples immediately, providing much faster cooling rate and O 2 -free condition than normal quenching treatment. ICP-OES is employed to determine the stoichiometric ratios of NAFMO materials ( Table S1 ), which are consistent to the designed composition. The SEM images ( Figs. S1a - c ) are collected to reveal the morphologies of the prepared materials, exhibiting similar irregular polyhedron shape and smooth surface facets. Moreover, all of the prepared materials present a uniform distribution of Al, Fe and Mn elements and are shown in the TEM and EDS mapping results ( Fig. S1d -o). Nevertheless, NAFMO-NC, NAFMOQU and NAFMO-LN materials present the median particle size (D50) of 932.6, 756.5 and 742.3 nm ( Fig. S2 ), respectively, indicating the pri mary particle sizes decrease and the bulk diffusion length reduces with the increasing of cooling rate. Additionally, the XRD, TOF PND and ssNMR results demonstrate that the prepared NAFMO materials through different cooling process present very distinct atomic occupancy, local structure and even long-range structure. As shown in Fig. 2 a and S3, both of NAFMO-NC and NAFMO-QU materials exhibit a pure hexagonal layered crystalline structure of P2 phase with space group of P6 3 /mmc (PDF#27–0751), while the NAFMO-LN sample presents a distorted P2 phase (denoted as P2 ’, PDF#27–0752), which includes the standard diffraction patterns of P2 phase and four new diffraction peaks. These four peaks are attributed to the orthorhombic distortion of P2 phase and the decreasing symmetry of crystal lattice, generating diffraction peaks at the relatively lower diffraction angle in comparison to the (110), (112), (113) and (114) peaks from P2 phase. Due to the ordered arrangement of TM ions during natural cooling process, two honeycomb-superstructure peaks appear at ~22 � and ~32 � of NAFMOLN sample [ 16 ]. Here, in order to further describe the degree of distortion, the definition of δ is showed in Fig. S4, which is consistent with previous report [ 30 ]. The ratio of lattice constant of NAFMO-LN is calculated to be b ortho /a ortho 1⁄4 1.799, suggesting a slight distortion compared to b ortho /a ortho 1⁄4 1.830 of the P2 ’ phase Na 0.67 MnO 2 (S.G. Cmcm ), and the degree of distortion, δ, is estimated to be 2.9% (for the P2 ’ phase, δ 1⁄4 8%) [ 30 ]. The high-quality TOF PND data were further collected to investigate the existence of cations vacancies in the TM layers for its highly sensitivity to the distribution of atoms [ 38 ]. Rietveld refinements were performed with GSAS [ 36 ] and the results of the refined fractional atomic coordinates and atomic occupancy based on XRD and ND are presented in Table S2 and Table S3, respectively, which are highly consistent with each other and with low Rwp values. These results reveal that the atomic occupancies of TM ions in the NAFMO-NC, -QU and -LN samples are 0.922, 0.964 and 1, corresponding to the TM vacancies of 0.078, 0.035 and 0 in the TM layers ( Fig. 2 b), respectively. Moreover, the full atomic occupancy of Mn ions is expected to generate the lowest Mn ions average valence to satisfy the charge balance in NAFMO materials, which leads to the unequal Mn - O bonds and ortho rhombic distortion in the NAFMO-LN sample ( Fig. 1 and Table S4 ). Besides, the lattice parameters a, c and V increase continuously with the rising cooling rate ( Fig. S3 ), due to the larger ions radius of Mn 3 þ (0.645 Å) than Mn 4 þ Besides, the lattice parameters a, c and V increase continuously with the rising cooling rate ( Fig. S3 ), due to the larger ions radius of Mn 3 þ (0.645 Å) than Mn 4 þ (0.53 Å). More importantly, as shown in Table S3, the continuously expanded extent of Na þ layers spacing indicates that the faster cooling treatment would facilitate the Na þ insertion and extrac tion. In summary, our above results reveal that the long-range structure of materials is highly relevant to the cooling process, and a faster cooling treatment can suppress the formation of vacancy more effectively and thus increasing atomic occupancy of Mn 4 þ /Mn 3 þ redox center in the TM layers. To thoroughly study the structure of the prepared materials, ss-NMR spectroscopy was employed, which is highly sensitive to the local and electronic environment of target nucleus. Previous works have concluded that the characteristic signal of P2 and P2 ’ phase with obvious orthorhombic distortion are at around 1250 and 850 ppm, respectively [ 34, 39 ]. Surprisingly, as presented in Fig. 2 c and S5, the 23 Na NMR spectra of the three materials only show one resonance signal at 1200–1450 ppm, even for the P2 ’ phase NAFMO-LN sample confirmed by XRD and TOF PND results. The results indicate that such a slight orthorhombic distortion of NAFMO-LN sample have no detectable effect on the local electronic environment of Na þ. Besides, all the spectra show a signal at around 0 ppm due to the diamagnetic sodium salts. Despite the chemical shifts and peak patterns for these three samples are Fig. 2. Structural characterization of Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.85 O 2. (a) Powder XRD patterns, (b) the atomic occupancy fitting results of TM ions from TOF PND data, and (c) 23 Na ss-NMR spectra of the prepared materials and the asterisks (*) indicate the spinning sidebands. (d - f) Rietveld refinements of TOF PND patterns for the asprepared samples. X. Liu et al. Nano Energy 76 (2020) 104997 5 similar, the full width at half maximum for NAFMO-NC (97.49 ppm) sample is significantly broader than that for NAFMO-QU (64.99 ppm) and NAFMO-LN (64.88 ppm) samples ( Fig. S5 ), which indicates that the uniformity of the Na þ local environment improves when accelerating the cooling rate. The local environment of Na þ is highly affected by the number of surrounding Mn ions though Na - O - Mn pathways and the presence of TM ions vacancies increases the types of local environment. Moreover, a higher content of paramagnetic Mn ions will cause a stronger Fermi-contact interaction around Na þ and thus a large chemi cal shift [ 40 ]. The resonance of NAFMO-QU sample locates at a larger chemical shift than that of the NAFMO-NC sample ( Fig. 2 c), which further confirm Mn atomic occupancy of NAFMO-QU is higher than that of NAFMO-NC sample. Additionally, the resonance shifts slightly to a lower chemical shift for the NAFMO-LN sample than NAFMO-QU sam ple, owing to the much longer Mn - O bonds in orthorhombic distortion phase ( Fig. 1 and Table S4 ) and consequently the Fermi-contact inter action around Na þ decreased. To further reveal the effects of different cooling rates on the Na þ (de) intercalation, dynamics of NAFMO-NC/QU/LN materials, T 2 (spin spin relaxation times) were obtained by the Hahn-echo method and the fitted results are showed in Fig. 3 a. For the paramagnetic solid-state materials, T 2 can eliminate the influence of transition metal ions and the faster Na þ hopping rate leads to longer T 2 value [ 41, 42 ]. As shown in Fig. 3 b, the T 2 value continuously increases with rising the cooling rate and reaches the highest value of 103 μ s for the NAFMO-LN sample, which is assigned to the largest Na þ layers spacing ( Table S4 ) and the fastest Na þ hopping rate. Besides, the electronic conductivity, as another important param eter for evaluating electrode materials, was measured by direct-current polarization method, and the results are showed in Fig. 3 c - d. The ob tained electronic conductivity of 3.812 x 10   5 S cm   1 for NAFMO-LN sample is higher than the NAFMO-QU sample (3.227 x 10   5 S cm   1 ) and much higher than NAFMO-NC sample (9.790 x 10   6 S cm   1 ), which may be attributed to the increasing contents of Mn 3 þ promoting electron hopping [ 37 ]. Thus, an excellent rate performance of NAFMO-LN sam ple is expected, due to the fastest Na þ mobility and highest electronic conductivity. The above results have shown that the long-range and local structure of materials are closely related to the cooling rate during the synthesis process. Next, the XRD patterns at variable temperature were performed for a better understanding of the structure evolution during cooling process. Specifically, the precursor was firstly heated up to 900 � C for 15 h in air, and then slowly cooled down to each temperature point. As shown in Fig. S6, the XRD patterns reveal that the material at 900 � C matches well with the standard patterns of P2 ’ phase, attributed to the low average valence of Mn ions thus leading to an orthorhombic distortion of P2 phase as commented before [ 29 ]. As the temperature decreases, the characteristic diffraction peaks (marked with “ y “ ) of P2 ’ phase are reduced in intensity and they are consecutively shifted to slightly higher diffraction angles due to the contraction of ab plane. The fact corresponds the disappearance of the orthorhombic distortion and the increasing of Mn ions average valence. As the temperature is cooled down to 800 � C and beneath, only P2 phase is observed and its diffraction signals fit perfectly the standard patterns of P2 phase, probably due to the fact that the crystal lattice tends to uptake oxygen in the cooling process [ 29 ]. The XRD data obtained at variable temperature fully reveal that the structure of the material subjected at different the cooling process [ 29 ]. The XRD data obtained at variable temperature fully reveal that the structure of the material subjected at different temperature during cooling process could be obtained by appropriately controlling the cooling rate, and the faster cooling treatment promote maintaining the structure of high-temperature (900 � C). 3.2. Electrochemical performance of Na 0.67 Al 0.1 Fe 0.05 Mn 0.85 O 2 cathode materials The electrochemical performance of the NAFMO-NC/QU/LN Fig. 3. (a - b) 23 Na NMR T 2 fitting results and (c - d) direct-current polarization fitting results of prepared materials. The spin-spin relaxation time (T 2 ) was measured by the Hahn-echo pulse sequence as a function of echo time, fitting by the formula of M xy 1⁄4 M xyo � (e -t/T2 ). Electronic conductivity is calculated by i ∞ and Ohm ’ s law. X. Liu et al. Nano Energy 76 (2020) 104997 6 materials was evaluated in Na half cells at 30 � C. The galvanostatic charge-discharge curves of the electrodes within the voltage range of 2.0–4.0 V at 12 mA g   1 (0.05C) current rate are shown in Fig. 4 a. As the cooling rate for the prepared materials increases, the following charac teristics of first cycle curves deserve special attention: (i) These three samples exhibit smooth charge-discharge profiles, confirming multiple phase transition and Na þ -vacancy ordering during (de)intercalation processes have been suppressed by the introduction of Al and Fe ele ments. While the plateaus at ~2.2 V and ~3.5 V become more obvious with increase of cooling rate, which are related to phase transition processes and will be discussed in the next section. (ii) The open circuit voltages ( Fig. S7 ) of the electrodes decrease continuously with the increasing of cooling rate, corresponding to the decrease of Mn ions average valence. (iii) The initial discharge capacities are 131, 175 and 202 mAh g   1 for NAFMO-NC, NAFMO-QU and NAFMO-LN, respec tively. They are significantly improved with the accelerating cooling rate. To the best our knowledge, NAFMO-LN material has shown the highest initial discharge capacity and specific energy density (~527 Wh kg   1, solely considering the mass of cathode active material) among reported P2-type sodium oxides materials in the voltage range of 2.0–4.0 V. Furthermore, as the working window is expanded to 1.5–4.5 V ( Fig. S8 ), the capacities delivered by NAFMO-NC, NAFMO-QU and NAFMO-LN are 210, 224 and 246 mA g   1, respectively, while an obvious plateau attributing to anionic redox reaction appears at ~4.2 V [ 17, 43, 44 ]. As indicated above, the anionic charge compensation pro cess usually shows the poor cyclabilities and slow dynamics in both sodium cathode materials and Li-analogues, so we mainly focus on the working potential below 4 V. In order to meet the needs of the market of smart grids, the requirement for the discharge rates of NIBs is much higher. The rate capabilities were tested by charging at low current and discharging at various currents, the same as previous researches [ 33, 35, 39 ], and the results are shown in Fig. 4 b - c. NAFMO-LN electrode shows the highest capacities and stable cyclabilities at each current density, providing the capacities of 202, 191, 173, 161 and 148 mAh g   1 as the discharge current densities are increased from 12, 24, 120, 240–480 mA g   1, respectively. Even with the 100 times increase of the current den sity (1200 mA g   1 ), the capacity retention is up to 60% (122 mAh g   1 ), indicating excellent rate capability among the reported high-rate P2-type electrodes. The diffusion coefficients of Na þ were calculated from GITT results to evaluate the (de)intercalation of kinetics of Na þ during first cycle. As shown in Fig. S9, the geomean D Na þ of NAFMO-LN (2.474 � 10   10 cm² s   1 ) is slight higher than NAFMO-QU (2.196 � 10   10 cm² s   1 ) and is much higher than that of NAFMO-NC (5.107 � 10   11 cm² s   1 ), which is consistent with the best rate performance of liquid N 2 treatment sample. In addition, the cycle stabilities of the ma terials are also investigated at different current densities of 12 and 1200 mA g   1 as shown in Fig. S10 and 4d, respectively. At the current density of 12 mA g   1, NAFMO-LN electrode delivers the highest initial discharge capacity of 202 mAh g   1 and the highest maintained capacity of 198 mAh g   1 after ten cycles without any electrode modifications. Espe cially, the excellent reversibility and negligible voltage change are found in the charge-discharge curves of NAFMO-LN electrodes. Due to the excellent rate performance of NAFMO-LN, this electrode was subjected to further galvanostatic charged-discharged cycles at such a high current rate of 1200 mA g   1 excellent rate performance of NAFMO-LN, this electrode was subjected to further galvanostatic charged-discharged cycles at such a high current rate of 1200 mA g   1. As shown in Fig. 4 d and S11, it also maintains the initial capacity of 122 mAh g   1 and the long cycling stability is achieved with the remarkable capacity retentions of 98% after 100 cycles, 95% after 200 cycles and 81% after 600 cycles at 1200 mA g   1 with ~100% coulombic efficiency during cycling. The plateaus of ~2.2 V and ~3.5 V are substantial reduction from the comparison dQ/dV curves at low (12 mA g   1 ) and high (1200 mA g   1 ) current densities, especially the plateau at low voltage which is deemed as the main reason for the ca pacity decays due to the notorious Mn 3 þ Jahn-Teller effects. Ex-situ 23 Na NMR was further performed to explore the structure evolutions at high current density. As shown in Fig. S12, the P2 phase signal is maintained during the whole charge process, and thus the electrode could exhibit Fig. 4.
